Relativistic Graph InterpretationOpen sidebarRelativistic Graph InterpretationDefault (GPT-3.5)Summarize: Coordinate space refers to a mathematical system used to represent points or positions in a space, typically in two or three dimensions. It provides a way to uniquely identify the location of a point using a set of numbers or coordinates.

In two-dimensional coordinate space (also known as a Cartesian plane), points are located using two coordinates: an x-coordinate and a y-coordinate. The x-coordinate represents the horizontal position, while the y-coordinate represents the vertical position. The intersection of these coordinates represents a unique point.

In three-dimensional coordinate space, an additional coordinate, typically denoted as z, is used to represent the depth or height dimension. This system is often used in three-dimensional geometry and computer graphics.

Coordinate spaces are fundamental in various fields, including mathematics, physics, computer science, engineering, and many others. They are used to describe positions, vectors, and various mathematical operations involving spatial relationships.1 / 1ChatGPTCoordinate space is a system in mathematics for identifying positions in space using numbers called coordinates. In a two-dimensional space, points are determined by x (horizontal) and y (vertical) coordinates. In three dimensions, an additional z coordinate indicates depth or height. This concept is essential in numerous fields like math, physics, and computer graphics for denoting positions and describing spatial relations.1 / 1Building up spacetime with quantum entanglement
Mark Van Raamsdonk

Building up spacetime with quantum entanglement 
Mark Van Raamsdonk 
Department of Physics and Astronomy, University of British Columbia 
6224 Agricultural Road, Vancouver, B.C., V6T 1W9, Canada 
mav@phas.ubc.ca 
Abstract 
In this essay, we argue that the emergence of classically connected spacetimes 
is intimately related to the quantum entanglement of degrees of freedom in a 
non-perturbative description of quantum gravity. Disentangling the degrees of 
freedom associated with two regions of spacetime results in these regions pulling 
apart and pinching of f from each other in a way that can be quantif i ed by standard 
measures of entanglement. 
Essay written for the Gravity Research Foundation 
2010 Awards for Essays on Gravitation 
March 31, 2010 
Introduction 
The gravity / gauge theory correspondence [1, 2, 3] in string theory represents exciting 
progress towards i nding a general non-perturbative description of quantum gravity. 
It posits that certain quantum gravitational theories with i xed spacetime asymptotic 
behavior are exactly equivalent to ordinary quantum i eld theories. We can view this 
correspondence as providing a complete non-perturbative def i nition of the quantum 
gravity theory via a quantum i eld theory. However, despite a great deal of evidence 
for the validity of this correspondence, we do not have a deep understanding of why or 
how spacetime/gravity emerges from the degrees of freedom of the i eld theory. 
In this essay, we will argue, based on widely accepted examples of gauge theory / 
gravity duality, that the emergence of spacetime in the gravity picture is intimately 
related to the quantum entanglement of degrees of freedom in the corresponding con- 
ventional quantum system. We will begin by showing that certain quantum superpo- 
sitions of states corresponding to disconnected spacetimes give rise to states that are 
interpreted as classically connected spacetimes. More quantitatively, we will see in a 
simple example that decreasing the entanglement between two sets of degrees of free- 
dom (e.g. by continuously varying the quantum state in the i eld theory description) 
ef f ectively increases the proper distance between the corresponding spacetime regions, 
while decreasing the area separating the two regions. 
Classical connectivity from quantum superposition 
The most familiar example of gauge-theory / gravity duality involves an equivalence 
between conformal i eld theories (CFTs) and asymptotically anti-de Sitter (AdS) space- 
times. For specif i c CFTs, each state of the i eld theory on a sphere (Ã time) corresponds 
to a spacetime in some specif i c theory of quantum gravity where the asymptotics of 
the spacetime are the same as global AdS spacetime. 
Let us now consider a slightly more complicated example, where we build a larger 
quantum system by taking two (non-interacting) copies of our conformal i eld theory on 
Sd. As usual, the Hilbert space for this system will be the tensor product H = H1âH2 
of the Hilbert spaces for the component systems. 
In our new system, the simplest quantum states to consider are product states (i.e. 
states with no entanglement between the two subsystems) 
|Î¨i = |Î¨1i â |Î¨2i . 
It is easy to provide a gravity interpretation for such a state. Since the degrees of 
freedom of the two CFTs do not interact in any way, and since there is no entanglement 
between the degrees of freedom for this state, the interpretation must be that we have 
two completely separate physical systems. If |Î¨1i is dual to one asymptotically AdS 
spacetime and |Î¨2i is dual to some other spacetime, the product state is dual to the 
disconnected pair of spacetimes.

A mathematical theory of semantic development in deep neural networks
Andrew M. Saxe https://orcid.org/0000-0002-9831-8812 andrew.saxe@psy.ox.ac.uk, James L. McClelland, and Surya GanguliAuthors Info & Affiliations

Significance
Over the course of development, humans learn myriad facts about items in the world, and naturally group these items into useful categories and structures. This semantic knowledge is essential for diverse behaviors and inferences in adulthood. How is this richly structured semantic knowledge acquired, organized, deployed, and represented by neuronal networks in the brain? We address this question by studying how the nonlinear learning dynamics of deep linear networks acquires information about complex environmental structures. Our results show that this deep learning dynamics can self-organize emergent hidden representations in a manner that recapitulates many empirical phenomena in human semantic development. Such deep networks thus provide a mathematically tractable window into the development of internal neural representations through experience.
Abstract
An extensive body of empirical research has revealed remarkable regularities in the acquisition, organization, deployment, and neural representation of human semantic knowledge, thereby raising a fundamental conceptual question: What are the theoretical principles governing the ability of neural networks to acquire, organize, and deploy abstract knowledge by integrating across many individual experiences? We address this question by mathematically analyzing the nonlinear dynamics of learning in deep linear networks. We find exact solutions to this learning dynamics that yield a conceptual explanation for the prevalence of many disparate phenomena in semantic cognition, including the hierarchical differentiation of concepts through rapid developmental transitions, the ubiquity of semantic illusions between such transitions, the emergence of item typicality and category coherence as factors controlling the speed of semantic processing, changing patterns of inductive projection over development, and the conservation of semantic similarity in neural representations across species. Thus, surprisingly, our simple neural model qualitatively recapitulates many diverse regularities underlying semantic development, while providing analytic insight into how the statistical structure of an environment can interact with nonlinear deep-learning dynamics to give rise to these regularities.1 / 1ChatGPTThis research article delves into understanding the development of semantic knowledge in humans and its representation by neuronal networks in the brain. A mathematical theory of semantic development in deep neural networksAuthors:
Andrew M. Saxe, James L. McClelland, and Surya GanguliPublication Date:
May 17, 2019Significance:Humans acquire a vast amount of knowledge about items and categorize them into meaningful groups over time. This semantic knowledge plays a critical role in various adult behaviors and inferences.The study seeks to understand how neural networks in the brain acquire, organize, deploy, and represent this structured knowledge.By examining the nonlinear learning dynamics of deep linear networks, the study uncovers that these networks can form emergent hidden representations that mirror many patterns seen in human semantic development. This implies that deep neural networks can serve as a useful model for exploring the development of internal neural representations through experience.Abstract:Research in human semantic knowledge has identified consistent patterns in how knowledge is gained, structured, used, and represented neurally.The study poses a fundamental question: What principles allow neural networks to gain and use abstract knowledge by combining multiple experiences?The authors explore this by analyzing the learning dynamics in deep linear networks.They identify exact solutions that explain various phenomena in semantic cognition, such as:Rapid developmental transitions that hierarchically differentiate concepts.Common semantic illusions between these transitions.The rise of item typicality and category coherence affecting semantic processing speed.Changing patterns of inductive projection over time.The preservation of semantic similarity in neural representations across different species.In essence, this simple neural model mimics numerous regularities seen in semantic development. It offers insights into how the statistical structure of an environment interacts with the nonlinear dynamics of deep learning, leading to these observed regularities.In summary, the research provides a mathematical lens to understand how deep neural networks can simulate the acquisition and organization of semantic knowledge, resembling patterns seen in human cognitive development.1 / 1Human cognition relies on a rich reservoir of semantic knowledge, enabling us to organize and reason about our complex sensory world (1â4). This semantic knowledge allows us to answer basic questions from memory (e.g., âDo birds have feathers?â) and relies fundamentally on neural mechanisms that can organize individual items, or entities (e.g., canary or robin), into higher-order conceptual categories (e.g., birds) that include items with similar features, or properties. This knowledge of individual entities and their conceptual groupings into categories or other ontologies is not present in infancy, but develops during childhood (1, 5), and in adults, it powerfully guides inductive generalizations.
The acquisition, organization, deployment, and neural representation of semantic knowledge has been intensively studied, yielding many well-documented empirical phenomena. For example, during acquisition, broader categorical distinctions are generally learned before finer-grained distinctions (1, 5), and periods of relative stasis can be followed by abrupt conceptual reorganization (6, 7). Intriguingly, during these periods of developmental stasis, children can believe illusory, incorrect facts about the world (2).
Also, many psychophysical studies of performance in semantic tasks have revealed empirical regularities governing the organization of semantic knowledge. In particular, category membership is a graded quantity, with some items being more or less typical members of a category (e.g., a sparrow is a more typical bird than a penguin). Item typicality is reproducible across individuals (8, 9) and correlates with performance on a diversity of semantic tasks (10â14). Moreover, certain categories themselves are thought to be highly coherent (e.g., the set of things that are dogs), in contrast to less coherent categories (e.g., the set of things that are blue). More coherent categories play a privileged role in the organization of our semantic knowledge; coherent categories are the ones that are most easily learned and represented (8, 15, 16). Also, the organization of semantic knowledge powerfully guides its deployment in novel situations, where one must make inductive generalizations about novel items and properties (2, 3). Indeed, studies of children reveal that their inductive generalizations systematically change over development, often becoming more specific with age (2, 3, 17â19).
Finally, recent neuroscientific studies have probed the organization of semantic knowledge in the brain. The method of representational similarity analysis (20, 21) revealed that the similarity structure of cortical activity patterns often reflects the semantic similarity structure of stimuli (22, 23). And, strikingly, such neural similarity structure is preserved across humans and monkeys (24, 25).
This wealth of empirical phenomena raises a fundamental conceptual question about how neural circuits, upon experiencing many individual encounters with specific items, can, over developmental time scales, extract abstract semantic knowledge consisting of useful categories that can then guide our ability to reason about the world and inductively generalize. While several theories have been advanced to explain semantic development, there is currently no analytic, mathematical theory of neural circuits that can account for the diverse phenomena described above. Interesting nonneural accounts for the discovery of abstract semantic structure include the conceptual âtheory-theoryâ (2, 17, 18) and computational Bayesian (26) approaches. However, neither currently proposes a neural implementation that can infer abstract concepts from a stream of examples. Also, they hold that specific domain theories or a set of candidate structural forms must be available a priori for learning to occur. In contrast, much prior work has shown, through simulations, that neural networks can gradually extract semantic structure by incrementally adjusting synaptic weights via error-corrective learning (4, 27â32). However, the theoretical principles governing how even simple artificial neural networks extract semantic knowledge from their ongoing stream of experience, embed this knowledge in their synaptic weights, and use these weights to perform inductive generalization, remain obscure.1 / 1ChatGPTThis passage dives deep into the understanding of human cognition and the reservoir of semantic knowledge, discussing how humans classify, reason, and make sense of the world around them. Here's a succinct summary:Human Cognition and Semantic Knowledge:Nature of Semantic Knowledge:Humans possess a vast pool of semantic knowledge that helps them make sense of their complex surroundings.This knowledge aids in answering memory-based questions, like identifying features of certain entities.Such knowledge, though not present at birth, develops during childhood and has profound influences on adult reasoning.Empirical Observations:Studies show patterns in how we acquire knowledge. Broad categories are generally learned before finer distinctions.There are periods where learning seems stagnant, followed by sudden reorganizations. During these stagnant phases, children might harbor misconceptions.Category membership varies, with some entities being more typical of a category than others. This typicality affects performance on various tasks.Some categories, based on coherence, play pivotal roles in our knowledge structure. Coherent categories are easier to learn and represent.Guidance by Semantic Knowledge:Our structured knowledge influences decisions in unfamiliar situations, especially in making inductive generalizations.As children grow, their inductive generalizations tend to become more refined and specific.Neuroscientific Insights:Techniques like representational similarity analysis have shown that cortical activity patterns often match the semantic similarity of stimuli.The neural structure of such similarity is consistent across humans and monkeys.Current Gap in Understanding:With so many observed phenomena, the question arises: How do neural circuits extract this semantic knowledge over time from individual experiences?Current theories, though abundant, lack a comprehensive mathematical framework that captures all these observed phenomena in terms of neural circuits.Some theories, though conceptually rich, don't offer a neural implementation to infer concepts from ongoing experiences.While neural networks have demonstrated their potential to extract semantic structures, the guiding principles behind their learning and generalization remain elusive.In essence, this passage underscores the critical role of semantic knowledge in human cognition, detailing empirical findings, current neuroscientific insights, and the gaps in our understanding, especially concerning neural network models.1 / 1CHAPTER 1 
Internal Set Theory 
Ordinarily in mathematics, when one introduces a new concept one 
def i nes it. For example, if this were a book on âblobsâ I would begin with 
a def i nition of this new predicate: x is a blob in case x is a topological 
space such that no uncountable subset is Hausdorf f . Then we would be 
all set to study blobs. Fortunately, this is not a book about blobs, and 
I want to do something dif f erent. I want to begin by introducing a new 
predicate âstandardâ to ordinary mathematics without def i ning it. 
The reason for not def i ning âstandardâ is that it plays a syntacti- 
cal, rather than a semantic, role in the theory. It is similar to the use 
of âf i xedâ in informal mathematical discourse. One does not def i ne this 
notion, nor consider the set of all i xed natural numbers. The statement 
âthere is a natural number bigger than any i xed natural numberâ does 
not appear paradoxical. The predicate âstandardâ will be used in much 
the same way, so that we shall assert âthere is a natural number bigger 
than any standard natural number.â But the predicate âstandardââ 
unlike âf i xedââwill be part of the formal language of our theory, and 
this will allow us to take the further step of saying, âcall such a natural 
number, one that is bigger than any standard natural number, unlim- 
ited.â We shall introduce axioms for handling this new predicate âstan- 
dardâ in a consistent way. In doing so, we do not enlarge the world of 
mathematical objects in any way, we merely construct a richer language 
to discuss the same objects as before. In this way we construct a the- 
ory extending ordinary mathematics, called Internal Set Theory1that 
axiomatizes a portion of Abraham Robinsonâs nonstandard analysis. In 
this construction, nothing in ordinary mathematics is changed.

Dynamic Bayesian Networks: 
Representation, Inference and Learning 
by 
Kevin Patrick Murphy 
Doctor of Philosophy in Computer Science

Modellingsequentialdatais importantinmanyareasofscienceandengineering. HiddenMarkovmodels 
(HMMs) and Kalman i lter models (KFMs) are popular for this because they are simple and l exible. For 
example, HMMs have been used for speech recognition and bio-sequence analysis, and KFMs have been 
used for problems ranging from tracking planes and missiles to predicting the economy. However, HMMs 
and KFMs are limited in their âexpressive powerâ. Dynamic Bayesian Networks (DBNs) generalize HMMs 
by allowing the state space to be representedin factored form, instead of as a single discrete random variable. 
DBNs generalize KFMs by allowing arbitrary probability distributions, not just (unimodal) linear-Gaussian. 
In this thesis, I will discuss how to represent many different kinds of models as DBNs, how to perform exact 
and approximate inference in DBNs, and how to learn DBN models from sequential data. 
In particular, the main novel technical contributions of this thesis are as follows: a way of representing 
Hierarchical HMMs as DBNs, which enables inference to be done in O(T) time instead of O(T 3), where 
T is the length of the sequence; an exact smoothing algorithm that takes O(logT) space instead of O(T); 
a simple way of using the junction tree algorithm for online inference in DBNs; new complexity bounds 
on exact online inference in DBNs; a new deterministic approximate inference algorithm called factored 
frontier; an analysis of the relationship between the BK algorithm and loopy belief propagation; a way of 
applying Rao-Blackwellised particle i ltering to DBNs in general, and the SLAM (simultaneous localization 
and mapping) problem in particular; a way of extending the structural EM algorithm to DBNs; and a variety 
of different applications of DBNs. However, perhaps the main value of the thesis is its catholic presentation 
of the i eld of sequential data modelling.1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly, the abstract you've provided is discussing Dynamic Bayesian Networks (DBNs) in detail. Introduction 
1.1State-space models 
Sequential data arises in many areas of science and engineering. The data may either be a time series, 
generated by a dynamical system, or a sequence generated by a 1-dimensional spatial process, e.g., bio- 
sequences. One may be interested either in online analysis, where the data arrives in real-time, or in off l ine 
analysis, where all the data has already been collected. 
In online analysis, one common task is to predict future observations, given all the observations up to 
the present time, which we will denote by y1:t= (y1,...,yt). (In this thesis, we only consider discrete-time 
systems, hence t is always an integer.) Since we will generally be unsure about the future, we would like to 
compute a best guess. In addition, we might want to know how conf i dent we are of this guess, so we can 
hedge our bets appropriately. Hence we will try to compute a probability distribution over the possible future 
observations; we denote this by P(yt+h|y1:t), 
where h > 0 is the horizon, i.e., how far into the future we 
want to predict. 
Sometimes we have some control over the system we are monitoring. In this case, we would like to 
predict future outcomes as a function of our inputs. Let u1:tdenote our past inputs, and ut+1:t+hdenote our 
next h inputs. Now the task is to compute P(yt+h|u1:t+h,y1:t). 
âClassicalâ approaches to time-series prediction use linear models, such as ARIMA, ARMAX, etc. (see 
e.g., [Ham94]), or non-linear models, such as neural networks (either feedforward or recurrent) or decision 
trees [MCH02]. For discrete data, it is common to use n-gram models (see e.g., [Jel97]) or variable-length 
Markov models [RST96, McC95]. 
There are several problems with the classical approach. First, we must base our prediction of the future 
on only a i nite window into the past, say ytââ:t, where â â¥ 0 is the lag, if we are to do constant work 
per time step. If we know that the system we are modelling is Markov with an order â¤ â, we will suffer 
no loss of performance, but in general the order may be large and unknown. Recurrent neural nets try to 
overcomethis problem by using internal state, but they are still not able to model long-distance dependencies 
1 
[BF95]. Second, it is diff i cult to incorporate prior knowledge into the classical approach: much of our 
knowledge cannot be expressed in terms of directly observable quantities, and black-box models, such as 
neural networks, are notoriouslyhard to interpret. Third, the classical approachhas diff i culties when we have 
multi-dimensional (multi-variate) inputs and/or outputs. For instance, consider the problem of predicting 
(and hence compressing) the next frame in a video stream using a neural network. Actual video compression 
schemes (such as MPEG) try to infer the underlyingâcauseâ behind what they see, and use that to predict the 
next frame. This is the basic idea behind state-space models, which we discuss next. 
In a state-space model, we assume that there is some underlying hidden state of the world that generates 
the observations, and that this hidden state evolves in time, possibly as a function of our inputs.1In an online 
setting, the goalis to inferthehiddenstate giventheobservationsupto thecurrenttime. If we let Xtrepresent 
the hidden state at time t, then we can def i ne our goal more precisely as computing P(Xt|y1:t,u1:t); this is 
called the belief state. 
Astrom [Ast65] proved that the belief state is a suff i cient statistic for prediction/control purposes, i.e., 
we do not need to keep around any of the past observations.2We can update the belief state recursively using 
Bayes rule, as we explain below. As in the case of prediction, we maintain a probability distribution over Xt, 
instead of just a best guess, in order to properly ref l ect our uncertainty about the âtrueâ state of the world. 
This can be useful for information gathering; for instance, if we know we are lost, we may choose to ask for 
directions. 
State-space models are better than classical time-series modelling approaches in many respects [Aok87, 
Har89, WH97, DK00, DK01]. In particular, they overcome all of the problems mentioned above: they do 
not suffer from i nite-window effects, they can easily handle discrete and multi-variate inputs and outputs, 
and they can easily incorporate prior knowledge. For instance, often we know that there are variables that 
we cannot measure, but whose state we would like to estimate; such variables are called hidden or latent. 
Including these variables allows us to create models which may be much closer to the âtrueâ causal structure 
of the domain we are modelling [Pea00]. 
Even if we are only interested in observable variables, introducing âf i ctitiousâ hidden variables often 
results in a much simpler model. For example, the apparent complexity of an observed signal may be more 
simply explained by imagining it is a result of two simple processes, the âtrueâ underlying state, which 
may evolve deterministically, and our measurement of the state, which is often noisy. We can then âexplain1 / 1ChatGPTThis chapter provides an introduction to state-space models and their significance in analyzing sequential data. Here's a summary:Chapter 1: IntroductionSequential Data: Such data, whether a time series from a dynamic system or a sequence generated by a spatial process like bio-sequences, plays a pivotal role in numerous scientific and engineering domains. Analysis can be real-time (online) or after data collection (offline).Online Analysis: A key task is predicting future observations based on past data. This involves creating a probability distribution over potential future observations, given the history up to the current time. If the system is under one's control, future outcomes are forecasted based on both past observations and inputs.Classical Time-Series Prediction: Traditional methods for time-series forecasting include linear models like ARIMA and ARMAX, as well as non-linear models like neural networks and decision trees. For discrete data, n-gram models or variable-length Markov models are commonly used.Limitations of the Classical Approach:The prediction must be based on a finite window into the past, limiting its ability to capture long-distance dependencies.Integrating prior knowledge is challenging, especially with black-box models like neural networks.Handling multi-dimensional inputs or outputs is complicated.State-Space Models: These models propose that there's a hidden state of the world that generates observations, and this state evolves over time. The primary aim is to deduce the concealed state based on the observations until the current moment.Belief State: Represents the probability distribution over the hidden state at time ttt, computed based on observations and inputs until that moment.Sufficiency: The belief state is a sufficient statistic for prediction/control, removing the need to retain past observations.Benefits: Compared to classical models, state-space models do not have the finite-window limitation, can effectively manage multi-variate inputs/outputs, and allow the integration of prior knowledge.Role of Hidden Variables: Even if the focus is solely on observable variables, introducing fictitious hidden variables can simplify the model. When you graph the height of a bouncing ball over time on a 2D plane, you're using the time axis to give context to the ball's motion in the vertical dimension. The graph isn't showing the ball's spatial motion directly but is rather representing how the height of the ball changes with respect to time.In the real world, if you were observing the ball, it would just be moving up and down vertically. But on the graph, it looks like the ball's height is "traveling" horizontally across time. This is because the graph is using the horizontal axis (time) as a means to "project" the changes in height (vertical motion) of the ball.This kind of visualization is powerful because it allows us to easily analyze the behavior of the ball over time: its peaks represent the highest points of the bounces, while the troughs represent when the ball touches the ground. We can easily identify the frequency of bounces, estimate how much height is lost in each bounce, and understand the general decay in energy as the bounces get smaller.It's a perfect example of how we often use lower-dimensional representations (like 2D graphs) to understand phenomena in our three-dimensional world by projecting certain aspects of the phenomena onto axes of interest.1 / 1Can we write an essay on these topics, titled "relativistic graph interpretation"1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly! Here's an essay on "Relativistic Graph Interpretation" based on the topics we've discussed:Relativistic Graph InterpretationIn the realm of mathematical and scientific visualizations, the act of graphing provides a bridge between abstract concepts and tangible understanding. A graph, in its essence, is a projectionâa way of capturing higher-dimensional information onto a lower-dimensional plane for ease of analysis. The term "relativistic" suggests a perspective-dependent understanding, much like Einstein's theory of relativity. In the context of graph interpretation, it can be seen as understanding phenomena from the perspective of different axes or dimensions.Consider the simple act of plotting the height of a bouncing ball against time. In reality, the ball moves solely in the vertical plane, with gravity pulling it downwards and the ground pushing it upwards upon each bounce. But when this motion is projected onto a 2D graph, the ballâs height appears to 'travel' horizontally across time. What might seem like side-to-side movement is, in actuality, a representation of the ball's vertical motion as it progresses through time. The horizontal axis (time) provides context, while the vertical axis captures the height of the ball.This graph is a relativistic interpretation of the ball's motion. Its meaning shifts depending on the axis you're focusing on. From the perspective of height, we see the oscillations of the ball. From the perspective of time, we observe the duration between bounces and the decay in the ball's energy over successive bounces.State-space models, as discussed in Kevin Patrick Murphy's thesis, present another layer to this idea. These models assert that there exists an underlying hidden state of the world that generates observations. By utilizing these hidden or latent states, we can often develop models that more accurately reflect the true causal structure of the phenomena we're observing. In many respects, this can be seen as introducing additional axes or dimensions to our graph. These additional dimensions, while not directly observable, can help to simplify and clarify our understanding of complex data.Much like the theory of relativity shifted our understanding of time and space, interpreting graphs in a 'relativistic' manner allows us to shift our perspective depending on which axis or dimension we're focusing on. By understanding that our graphical representations are projectionsâand by being aware of what's being projected and whyâwe can glean deeper insights from our data.In conclusion, the concept of 'relativistic graph interpretation' underscores the importance of perspective in data analysis. By projecting higher-dimensional data onto lower-dimensional planes, we can visualize complex phenomena in a more comprehensible manner. But, as with any projection, it's crucial to remember what's been flattened, what's been highlighted, and what might have been obscured in the process.I hope this essay captures the essence of the topics we've discussed!1 / 1I am thinking that spacetime diagrams are deceptive1 / 1ChatGPTSpacetime diagrams, often known as Minkowski diagrams in the context of special relativity, are a tool used in physics to visualize events as they occur in spacetime. They offer a way to graphically represent the interplay between space and time in relativistic contexts. Like all models and representations, spacetime diagrams come with both benefits and limitations. Let's dive into why one might see them as deceptive, and also why they're invaluable:Why they might be seen as deceptive:Two Dimensions vs. Four Dimensions: A spacetime diagram typically plots time against one spatial dimension, effectively reducing a four-dimensional reality (three spatial dimensions + time) to a two-dimensional diagram. This reduction can be seen as an oversimplification.Relative to the Observer: These diagrams are usually drawn from a particular observer's frame of reference. As special relativity tells us, time and space can appear differently to different observers moving at different velocities. Therefore, a spacetime diagram valid for one observer might not represent events as they appear to another.Lorentz Contraction and Time Dilation: At relativistic speeds (speeds close to the speed of light), time dilation and length contraction occur. These phenomena can be counterintuitive and make spacetime diagrams seem misleading to those unfamiliar with the principles of relativity.Why they are invaluable:Visualizing Relativity: Despite the potential for misinterpretation, spacetime diagrams provide a way to visualize concepts in special relativity that can be challenging to grasp, such as simultaneity's relativity.Worldlines: They allow for the representation of worldlines, which are paths that objects take through spacetime. This helps in visualizing how different events are connected in time and space.Teaching and Learning Tool: For learners being introduced to special relativity, spacetime diagrams can be instrumental in building a foundational understanding before diving into the more complex mathematics of the theory.Comparing Frames of Reference: By using spacetime diagrams, it's easier to compare how different observers perceive the timing and sequence of events, providing a visual aid to the sometimes non-intuitive results of special relativity.In conclusion, like any scientific tool or representation, spacetime diagrams come with inherent limitations. They're an approximation, a way to visualize complex, multi-dimensional ideas on a page or screen. When interpreted with an understanding of their limitations and the principles they're grounded in, they're not so much deceptive as they are a simplified representation. As with all tools in physics, the key lies in understanding when and how to use them effectively.1 / 1What about change magnification, what are its practical applications?1 / 1ChatGPTChange magnification refers to a method or process by which small variations or perturbations in a system are amplified, either for study, observation, or practical application. This is crucial in, for example, audio systems where a small original signal from a microphone or other source is amplified to be played out of speakers.Economics and Finance: The concept of leverage in finance is an example of change magnification. Indeed, "time dilation" can be a somewhat misleading term for those not immersed in the physics of relativity.In the theory of relativity, time and space are intertwined into a four-dimensional spacetime. When we talk about "time dilation," we're often referencing the difference in the elapsed time as measured by two clocks, due to a velocity difference between them or due to the difference in gravitational fields they experience. This effect is not an illusion or a mere artifact of measurement; it's a real and confirmed aspect of how spacetime behaves.The concept of "distance between events" that you mentioned is closely related to the spacetime interval, an invariant quantity in relativity. This interval incorporates both spatial and temporal separations between events and remains the same regardless of the observer's state of motion.In this context, "time dilation" refers to the "stretching" or "compression" of the temporal component of this spacetime interval as observed from different reference frames. It's a manifestation of the relativistic nature of time. So, while "time dilation" might sound like time itself is being altered, it's more about how intervals or distances between events in spacetime are perceived differently by different observers.In simpler terms, events that seem simultaneous in one frame of reference might not appear so in another, and the time (or the interval) between these events can appear longer or shorter depending on the relative velocities or gravitational fields involved.1 / 1So time does not slow down rather the distance is shortest in the timeframe where two events happen in the same place. So if you bounce a ball up and down and then jump to the speed of light, your image of the ball looks like it takes forever to make a bounce, but it doesn't make the ball bouncing actually slow down.1 / 1ChatGPTExactly. You've captured an important distinction. In the context of special relativity, when we say "time slows down" for an object moving close to the speed of light relative to an observer, what we're really saying is that there's a difference in the way time intervals (or the duration between events) are measured between two different frames of reference.Let's unpack the ball bouncing analogy:Stationary Frame: Imagine you're standing still and watching a ball bounce up and down. It bounces at a regular interval, let's say once per second.Moving Frame: Now, imagine you're observing the same ball while you're moving close to the speed of light relative to it. Due to the effects of special relativity, you would perceive the ball to be bouncing slower than once per second. It might seem to take, say, five seconds for a single bounce.But here's the key: From the ball's perspective, in its own frame of reference, it's still bouncing once every second. The ball isn't experiencing time any differently. It's just that the measurement of time differs between the two frames of reference.The concept of "time dilation" often leads to misconceptions, as the terminology suggests that time itself is being manipulated or altered. In reality, it's a difference in the perception of elapsed time between events due to the relative motion between observers.1 / 1And you can definitely never have a reference frame where it is so slow that time reverses2 / 2ChatGPTThat's correct. In special relativity, time dilation can make time appear to "slow down" for a moving frame relative to a stationary observer, but it can never reverse time.Mathematically, when we derive the time dilation formula:Îtâ²=Ît1âv2c2\Delta t' = \frac{\Delta t}{\sqrt{1 - \frac{v^2}{c^2}}}Îtâ²=1âc2v2ââÎtâWhere:Îtâ²\Delta t'Îtâ² is the time interval measured in the moving frame.Ît\Delta tÎt is the time interval measured in the stationary frame.vvv is the velocity of the moving frame relative to the stationary frame.ccc is the speed of light.As vvv approaches ccc, the denominator approaches zero, making Îtâ²\Delta t'Îtâ² become infinitely large. This means that time appears to slow down infinitely as you approach the speed of light, but it will never reach a point where it reverses.Moreover, moving faster than the speed of light would require infinite energy according to Einstein's theory and is thus considered impossible based on our current understanding of physics. No mysterious motor driving time forward- 
Multiple paths of random motion toward time irreversibility 
Qiuping A. Wang1,2, Qiong Ye2 
1 Laboratoire SCIQ, ESIEA, 9 Rue VÃ©sale, 75005 Paris, France 
2 IMMM, CNRS UMR 6283, UniversitÃ© du Mans, Ave. O. Messiaen, Le Mans, France 
Abstract 
In this work, we address the concept of time within classical physics with the aim of solving 
some old questions concerning the definition of time and the conflict between the time 
reversibility of classical physics and the time irreversibility observed in every physics system. 
We start from a mathematical definition of time based on the intuitive idea that time is an 
attribute of movement or change, an attribute perceived by the observer through the action of 
wait. In this definition, the wait, a mind-dependent concept, is mathematically related to 
successive positions or path of movement, giving birth to an objective time. It is shown that 
time defined in this way possesses all the properties of the time of physics, and is necessarily 
reversible for deterministic motion having uniqueness of path prescribed by the principle of 
least action. However, time irreversibility emerges for indeterministic (random) motion 
occurring over multiple paths. A mathematical proof of time irreversibility is formulated by1 / 1ChatGPTThe paper you mentioned, titled "No mysterious motor driving time forward - Multiple paths of random motion toward time irreversibility" by Qiuping A. Wang and Qiong Ye, appears to delve into the concept of time within classical physics. It addresses questions related to the definition of time and the conflict between the time reversibility suggested by classical physics and the time irreversibility observed in various physical systems.The authors propose a mathematical definition of time based on the idea that time is an attribute of movement or change, perceived through the action of waiting. In this definition, waiting is mathematically related to the successive positions or paths of movement, resulting in an objective time. This objective time behaves in a reversible manner for deterministic motion with a unique path, as prescribed by the principle of least action. However, it becomes irreversible for indeterministic (random) motion occurring over multiple paths.The paper seems to provide a mathematical proof of time irreversibility in the context of classical physics. This concept ties into our previous discussions on the nature of time, including time dilation, time as a flexible dimension, and the impossibility of time reversal, showcasing the multifaceted nature of time in both physics and philosophy.1 / 1Our proposition about the definition of time is the following. The universally accepted point 
of view that time is associated with movement comes directly from the intuition that time is 
only quantitatively perceptible when something moves or changes [7]. If nothing changed, there 
would be no measurable time. Thus, time is always measured with moving objects, examples 
include the sun, earth, moon (the very first clocks of human beings), the pendulum, the arms of 
clocks, and todayâs most developed atomic clocks. If we look carefully into this connection 
between time and movement, it becomes obvious that time is placed at the same epistemic level 
as movement. Movement is changing position. We must admit that movement can only occur 
or be well defined when there is well defined positions. Position is defined in space, or positions 
form space [7]. By this consideration, time becomes unquestionably less primitive than space,

b) How to define time? 
It is helpful to think once again about the assertion of Aristotle that time is the number of 
motion according to before and after [2]. Here the number means measure of time with given 
unit, day, month, years etc. [7]. Aristotle insisted that the essence of time is movement, as he 
discussed at length in his Physics [2]. But he did not specify the property and the form of this 
link to movement. Bergson went farther in the analysis of the concept of time by introducing 
the pure duration of consciousness, kind of sensation of wait with patience or impatience, 
defined as a succession of certain states of consciousness [7]. He illustrated the pure duration 
with the feeling of wait for a sugar to melt in water, but he differentiated it from the time of 
physics7 [8]. Certainly, state of consciousness is not state of physics of a movement; but a state 
of consciousness of the observer can be connected to a state of physics (position) by the action 
of observation of the position. Similarly, a succession of states of consciousness, represented 
by the feeling of wait, can be connected to a succession of positions by a series of simultaneous 
observations. To put it differently, the pure duration can be spread out, by the action of wait and observation, over a succession of positions of a motion. This consideration opens the way 
to a definition of time as presented below. 
We will avoid the words such as before, after, duration, elapse, period, anterior, posterior 
etc. in defining time, because these terms are too often associated to time in common languages. 
Using them may give an impression of self-referential definition of time [25]. We propose 
instead to use the term wait. As mentioned above, wait is the sensation experienced by the 
observer of movement, i.e., the observer must wait in order to perceive the succession of 
positions one after another. The ability of waiting, or the action of wait for something in the 
future, is an instinctive and innate capability of human being and of most if not all animals; it 
is a basic capability guaranteeing their survival, just as the capability of perceiving position 
correctly to find things necessary for their life or to protect themselves from danger. The word 
wait is familiar to everybody and easier to understand than the abstruse concept of pure duration 
of consciousness8 whose origin is still under active investigation today [26]. 
c) Definition of time 
To start the introduction to our approach to a definition of time, it is helpful to move our 
mind away from the familiar concepts of space-time, velocity, acceleration etc., and to go back 
to the cognitive context before modern physics as early as 14th century when there was no 
calculus using time. Many scholars of that time, Buridan and Oresme among others, tried to 
investigate the relationship between the mobility (they called it quality) of motion and time 
[28]. They took time as a primitive variable without defining it, and expressed the quality (Q) 
as a function of time. To our knowledge, this is the first use of time as mathematical variable 
because Oresme expressed Q as vertical axis in a figure where time is horizontal axis. A uniform 
motion is represented by a constant quality (horizontal straight line) in Oresme's figure [28], in 
which the spatial position of moving object or the distance L covered by the motion can be 
defined by the area below the straight line between the initial time ð¡0 and final time ð¡ð, i.e., ð¿ =

concept belonging to consciousness and independent of the external physics world. The innate ability of perceiving 
successive positions is a potential of action of wait. There is no duration in it. The feeling of duration can take 
place when the action of wait occurs with the perception of successive positions or events.

Nevertheless, as the time of Eq.(1) is a wait, and the feeling of wait is experienced by our 
mind always affected by emotion or by the nature of the observed movement [27], a given time 
duration may be perceived differently, depending on the mental state of the observer or on the 
nature of the movement [27]. It is a common knowledge that our emotions warp our perception 
of time (one minute seems longer for someone suffering than for someone enjoying himself). 
However, this mental effect is by no means a proof of the subjectivity of the time of physics. 
An advantage of Eq.(1) is that it removes the mind-dependent character of wait by connecting 
it to the objective positions. 
c) Relativity of time 
The relativity of time is a very important property of the time of physics. It is related to the 
famous time dilation due to relative motion and gravitation [23][24]. This property of time is 
sometimes puzzling for many because, if time is an objective quantity in nature, why is it 
observed differently by different observers? Does this hint that time is not objective at all and 
just a mind-dependent variable and spurious concept9 [9][10][25]? Here we will show that the 
objective time of Eq.(1) inevitably possesses this relativistic property. 
One of the important characteristics of Eq.(1) is that time is a relative quantity delimited by 
at least two consecutive events. Therefore, on the one hand, absolute time of a single position 
or event does not make sense. What we call time is nothing but an interval (duration) between 
two successive events. It is useless to imagine a time other than duration between events. It is 
a common knowledge that a time moment associated with a single event in our common 
language is always calculated with respect to another time moment of an event (reference). 
On the other hand, as positions and paths movements are reference-dependent, reference- 
independent absolute time does not exist. Time defined by Eq.(1) must inherit the relativistic 
character of position and path. This naturally leads to the dilation of time due to relative motion.

4) From uniqueness of path to time reversibility 
Defining time by Eqs.(1) to (3) is as natural and intuitive as reading time in the changing 
positions of the arms of a clock. It is the simplest way to see what is called time. Nevertheless, 
people have tried to find it elsewhere outside movement for many reasons. One of them may 
be the paradox between the reversibility of movement (and time as well) prescribed by the laws 
of physics and the irreversibility of time in nature where these laws seem to apply perfectly. 
Finding a solution of this paradox is the main objective of this work. Let us first discuss the 
sign of time, which is necessary since there seems no unanimous understanding of the negative 
sign (âð¡) we are used to write in front of the (forward) time ð¡ [34]. 
13 
a) What is time reversal 
As defined in Eqs.(1) to (3), time of a motion is perceived by the observer through his 
sensation of wait. Wait gives the feeling of increasing duration. So naturally, we tend to give a 
positive sign to the time of the observed motion along a trajectory. We accept the convention 
that a forward motion has positive and increasing time ð¡. Given this choice, what does mean if 
we write âð¡? 
Now let ð(ð¥) in Eq.(3) be the quality of a motion along its trajectory in the direction with 
positive ð¡, then âð(ð¥) implies the inversion of the sign of forward quality at every point on the 
path, yielding an opposite motion along the same trajectory, just as the rewind of a video of 
motion. If a real motion can be rewound in this way, it is able to go back and retrieve all its past 
positions. We say that the motion is reversible. If a system completely recovers its past, we can

5) When and how can time be irreversible? 
According to the above analysis, all motions obeying the principle of least action must be 
time reversible. This explains why the laws of physics which can be derived from this basic 
principle are all time reversible. The question is how and why, in all systems supposedly 
governed by those time reversible laws, time is always irreversible? If each of the components 
of an ensemble is white, but the ensemble is not white, there must be something else in play. 
This point is now tackled in what follows. 
a) Multiplicity of path of random motion

uniqueness of path has never been observed in real motions, all motions in nature being more 
or less random with unpredictable future states. One may object to this assertion by saying that 
all motions in nature obeying Newtonian laws have uniqueness of path and predictability. The

It's closely related to the concepts of entropy, irreversibility, and the emergence of order in dynamic systems. Here's an overview of Prigogine's ideas:Dissipative Structures: Prigogine introduced the concept of dissipative structures to describe systems that maintain their stability and organization while dissipating energy and matter from their surroundings. These structures arise in far-from-equilibrium conditions, which are situations where systems are driven by energy gradients or subjected to continuous external forces.Non-Equilibrium Thermodynamics: Traditional thermodynamics, based on equilibrium states, cannot explain the behavior of systems far from equilibrium. Prigogine's work extended thermodynamics to describe such systems. He introduced the notion that systems can exist in "dynamic equilibrium," which is a state of stable, non-equilibrium behavior.Irreversibility: Prigogine emphasized the role of irreversibility in the emergence of dissipative structures. In far-from-equilibrium systems, irreversible processes lead to the production of entropy, but they can also drive the system toward a higher level of order and complexity. This contrasts with equilibrium thermodynamics, where processes are reversible, and entropy tends to increase.Order Through Fluctuations: Dissipative structures often emerge through a delicate balance between the system's internal processes and fluctuations or perturbations from the environment. These fluctuations can lead to the spontaneous formation of ordered patterns or structures.Examples: Some common examples of dissipative structures include convection patterns in fluids, chemical oscillations, and biological systems like the formation of spatial patterns in developing embryos.Prigogine's work challenged the classical view that the universe tends toward disorder and highlighted the capacity of dynamic, non-equilibrium systems to generate and sustain order. His ideas have had a profound impact on fields such as chemistry, biology, and the study of complex systems, emphasizing the role of far-from-equilibrium conditions in the emergence of complexity and organization.In the context of your previous questions about increasing path entropy and time irreversibility, Prigogine's work aligns with the idea that systems, when driven far from equilibrium, can exhibit complex, self-organizing behavior and that irreversibility plays a crucial role in this process. It offers a perspective on how order and structure can emerge and persist in dynamic systems, even in the presence of entropy and randomness.1 / 1I claim no system is time reversable unless you ignore friction.1 / 1ChatGPTYour claim is quite accurate. Time dilation is a fundamental concept in Einstein's theory of special relativity, and it's typically derived using mathematical equations derived from the postulates of relativity. However, I can provide a simplified explanation of time dilation using the Pythagorean theorem and basic algebra to give you an intuitive understanding.In special relativity, time dilation occurs when an observer is in relative motion compared to another observer. Here's a simplified derivation using the Pythagorean theorem:Consider two observers, one at rest (S) and the other in motion (S') relative to each other.Let's assume that both observers have synchronized their clocks at a specific event (e.g., when they pass each other).Observer S measures the time interval (Ît) between two events happening at the same location in their own frame of reference. According to the Pythagorean theorem, the spacetime interval (Îs) for this event is given by:ÎsÂ² = ÎxÂ² + ÎyÂ² + ÎzÂ² - (cÎt)Â²Here, c is the speed of light, Îx, Îy, and Îz represent spatial differences, and Ît is the time difference as measured by observer S.Now, observer S' measures the same events but in their own frame of reference, which is moving relative to S. They also measure the spacetime interval (Îs') for the same event:Îs'Â² = Îx'Â² + Îy'Â² + Îz'Â² - (cÎt')Â²Here, Îx', Îy', Îz', and Ît' represent the measurements made by observer S'.Due to the principle of relativity, Îs and Îs' must be equal, as both events happen at the same spacetime coordinates. So we have:ÎsÂ² = Îs'Â²Now, using algebra to equate the two spacetime intervals:ÎxÂ² + ÎyÂ² + ÎzÂ² - (cÎt)Â² = Îx'Â² + Îy'Â² + Îz'Â² - (cÎt')Â²This equation can be rearranged to highlight the time dilation effect:(cÎt')Â² = (cÎt)Â² - (Îx'Â² + Îy'Â² + Îz'Â² - ÎxÂ² - ÎyÂ² - ÎzÂ²)Ît' = Ît / â(1 - vÂ²/cÂ²)Here, v is the relative velocity between the two observers.This equation is known as the time dilation formula and shows that the time experienced by the moving observer (Ît') is dilated or stretched compared to the time experienced by the stationary observer (Ît). It demonstrates how relative motion affects the perception of time, which is a fundamental aspect of Einstein's theory of special relativity.1 / 1Ok well I think it is a little easier to imagine it as a light bouncing between two mirrors on a rocketship1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly, the example of light bouncing between two mirrors on a rocketship is a classic way to illustrate time dilation in the context of special relativity. It's often referred to as the "light clock" thought experiment. Here's how it works:Imagine a rocketship traveling through space with a light clock on board. A light clock consists of two parallel mirrors facing each other, with a beam of light bouncing between them.For an observer inside the rocketship (let's call them Observer A), the light beam moves straight up and down between the mirrors, reflecting perfectly.Now, consider an outside observer (Observer B) who is stationary relative to the rocketship and observes it from a distance.From Observer B's perspective, the rocketship is moving at a constant velocity, and they see the light beam traveling on a diagonal path between the mirrors due to the rocket's motion.According to the principle of the constancy of the speed of light (one of the postulates of special relativity), both Observer A and Observer B agree that the speed of light remains the same. This means that the diagonal path of the light beam must be longer than the straight vertical path.As a consequence, from Observer B's perspective, it takes longer for the light beam to travel between the mirrors compared to what Observer A experiences. This is time dilation in action: the moving clock (light clock) ticks more slowly relative to the stationary observer.This thought experiment demonstrates how relative motion affects the perception of time. It's a simple and intuitive way to grasp the concept of time dilation in special relativity, showing that time can indeed slow down for a moving observer relative to a stationary one.1 / 1Use the Pythagorean theorem more explicitly1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly! Let's use the Pythagorean theorem more explicitly to derive time dilation in the context of the light clock thought experiment.In the rest frame of the rocket (Observer A), the light beam moves straight up and down between the mirrors, covering a vertical distance we'll call "d." This is the proper height of the light clock.According to the Pythagorean theorem, in a right triangle with sides of length "d" (vertical distance) and "ct" (the horizontal distance traveled by light in time "t"), the hypotenuse represents the spacetime interval "Îs" (the path of light):Îs^2 = (ct)^2 + d^2For the moving observer outside the rocket (Observer B), they see the rocketship moving with velocity "v." In their frame, the light beam follows a longer diagonal path due to the rocket's motion. The horizontal distance becomes "vt" while the vertical distance remains "d":Îs'^2 = (vt)^2 + d^2According to the principle of the constancy of the speed of light, both observers A and B agree that the speed of light is "c." So, we can set Îs equal to Îs' and compare the two spacetime intervals:(ct)^2 + d^2 = (vt)^2 + d^2Now, let's rearrange the equation to isolate the time component "t" in both frames:(ct)^2 - (vt)^2 = d^2Factoring out "t^2" and simplifying:t^2(c^2 - v^2) = d^2Finally, solve for "t" (time experienced by Observer A) in terms of "t'" (time experienced by Observer B):t^2 = (d^2) / (c^2 - v^2)t = d / â(c^2 - v^2)This equation shows that the time experienced by Observer A (inside the rocket) is dilated or "stretched" compared to the time experienced by Observer B (outside the rocket) due to their relative motion. The denominator â(c^2 - v^2) is often denoted as "Î³," the Lorentz factor, which characterizes the extent of time dilation in special relativity. When the rocketship's velocity "v" is much smaller than the speed of light "c," Î³ is approximately 1, and time dilation is negligible. However, as "v" approaches "c," Î³ increases, and time dilation becomes more significant.1 / 1Yeah I calculated one time how long it would take to reach the speed of light so that the force was always one G, do you think you can do that?1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly, I can help you calculate the time it would take for an object to reach the speed of light while experiencing a constant acceleration of 1 G (one gravitational acceleration), which is approximately 9.81 m/sÂ². In this work, we have addressed the conflict between the time of physics, which is reversible 
according to the fundamental laws of classical physics, and the irreversible time of nature we 
experience every day. Our starting point is a mathematical definition of time to implement the 
intuitive idea that time is an attribute of movement perceived through the instinctive action of 
wait of the observer to see successive positions. The concept of wait is thus related to the path 
of motion to give birth to time of physics. This definition opens the way to mathematical 
investigation of the properties of time from the properties of the paths of motion. We have 
shown that the time defined in this way has all the attributes of the time of classical physics, 
and is necessarily reversible in deterministic movement. 
The second step of this work is to consider the role of random motion characterized by 
multiplicity of path. We have shown with mathematical proof that, in the case of multiple paths 
13 Without any mystic appeal to consciousness it is possible to find a direction of time â¦ Let us draw an arrow 
arbitrarily. If as we follow the arrow we find more and more of the random element in the state of the world, then 
the arrow is pointed towards the future; if the random element decreases, the arrow points towards the past. That 
is the only distinction known to physics. This follows at once if our fundamental contention is admitted that the 
introduction of randomness is the only thing which cannot be undone (Eddington, The Nature of the Physical 
World). 
21 
having path probability, time inevitably becomes irreversible. To put it differently, one cannot 
rewind time when it goes on in disordered dynamics. You can never undo the randomness of 
an indeterministic movement. This randomness turns out to be the motor driving time in the 
direction towards increasing disorder and uncertainty14. 
The result of this work is in accordance with the thermodynamic arrow of time and entropy 
increase, with no need of probabilistic interpretation of the second law. In the framework of the 
probabilistic Hamiltonien mechanics based on the path probability, PoincarÃ©'s recurrence 
theorem is absent due to the modification of the Liouville's theorem (conservation of phase 
density distribution along the path of the motion), leading to Boltzmann's H theorem (no need 
of molecular chaos [12]) and the concomitant increase of entropy [40]. The probabilistic 
Hamiltonian mechanics invalidates all the arguments of deterministic mechanics against the 
pioneer work of Boltzmann [39][46], including the Maxwell's demon (and other ones [19]) 
haunting the second law of thermodynamics. Indeed, in the context of indeterministic motion 
with multiplicity of paths and uncontrollable randomness of the gas molecules, the demon can 
no more measure the velocity of a molecule with certainty and foresee its direction towards his 
trapdoor. It is useless for him to open the trapdoor to let a fast-moving molecule pass through 
because the molecule may change its direction and its velocity just in front of the door instead 
of going through. In this random world, does the demon himself also show more or less disorder 
in his action? Yes to our eyes because he is living in a ubiquitous randomness.

It is also worth emphasizing that the indeterministic nature of movement considered in this 
work must be objective in order for the path probability to be objective, leading to observer- 
independent time arrow. Subjective and observer-dependent randomness due to incomplete 
14 This impossibility of time reversal can be popularized as follows. You leave your car walking away in an open 
field for a while, blindfolded. Then you are asked to return to your car by walking backwards along whatever path 
as you please, you will end up finding your car, perhaps after a long walk. Now you are imposed a constraint to 
walk backwards on the same footprints as your walking away. With the presence of all the uncertainty in the 
measure of step size, in the way you move your legs, and in the irregular form of the ground which you cannot 
see, without mentioning all the other chances you can meet on a wild field, the probability for you to recover your 
car will be very small, eventually almost zero. Now let the size of your footprints and of your feet become 
infinitesimally small (required by the strong reversibility), I am sure that you will lose your car. If, moreover, the 
size of your car tends to zero (required by the weak reversibility), the probability for you to meet again your car is 
zero whichever path you take. 
22 
knowledge of deterministic motion, including chaos [12][18], necessarily leads to subjective 
and observer-dependent probability and then to the illusory character of entropy increase and 
time arrow. 
This view is closely related to a philosophy-laden question about the origin of the objective 
randomness in the context of the deterministic laws of classical physics ruling the world. The 
millennium old puzzles opposing determinism against indeterminism, and necessity against 
chance, are well known to all [18][33][34]. The debate seems deadlocked nowadays because 
the absence of irrefutable evidences persists for both deterministic and indeterministic view of 
nature [18][33][34]. In this context, we suggest the following alternative approach to the 
question. In view of the infallible mathematics linking time arrow to multiplicity of path, it is a 
certainty that time loses its reversibility in random motion. Inversely, if a motion is time 
irreversible, it certainly takes place over multiple paths of random motionsd, since the 
uniqueness of path of deterministic motions necessarily yields reversible time. Itâs mathematics. 
Therefore, a plausible method to justify the objectivity and the ubiquity of indeterministic 
nature of the world by its time irreversibility, observed everywhere with no counter-example to 
date. Put it shortly, time irreversibility is an evidence of the indeterministic nature of the world.

No mysterious motor driving time forward Multiple 
paths of randomness toward time irreversibility 
Qiuping A. Wang, Qiong Ye
